href="#other_reviews">\n    <meta itemprop="reviewCount" content="6022" />\n    6,022\n    reviews\n</a>\n</div>\n\n<div id="descriptionContainer">\n  \n      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">\n        \n<span id="freeTextContainer14708463587167722882">If you want to build a better future, you must believe in secrets.<br/><br/>The great secret of our time is that there are still uncharted frontiers to explore and new inventions to create. In Zero to One, legendary entrepreneur and investor Peter Thiel shows how we can find singular ways to create those new things. <br/><br/>Thiel begins with the contrarian premise that we live in an age of </span>\n  <span id="freeText14708463587167722882" style="display:none">If you want to build a better future, you must believe in secrets.<br /><br />The great secret of our time is that there are still uncharted frontiers to explore and new inventions to create. In Zero to One, legendary entrepreneur and investor Peter Thiel shows how we can find singular ways to create those new things. <br /><br />Thiel begins with the contrarian premise that we live in an age of technological stagnation, even if we\xe2\x80\x99re too distracted by shiny mobile devices to notice. Information technology has improved rapidly, but there is no reason why progress should be limited to computers or Silicon Valley. Progress can be achieved in any industry or area of business. It comes from the most important skill that every leader must master: learning to think for yourself.<br /><br />Doing what someone else already knows how to do takes the world from 1 to n, adding more of something familiar. But when you do something new, you go from 0 to 1. The next Bill Gates will not build an operating system. The next Larry Page or Sergey Brin won\xe2\x80\x99t make a search engine. Tomorrow\xe2\x80\x99s champions will not win by competing ruthlessly in today\xe2\x80\x99s marketplace. They will escape competition altogether, because their businesses will be unique. <br /><br />Zero to One presents at once an optimistic view of the future of progress in America and a new way of thinking about innovation: it starts by learning to ask the questions that lead you to find value in unexpected places.</span>\n  <a data-text-id="14708463587167722882" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n      </div>\n</div>\n\n    <div id=buyButtonContainer class="uitext buttons buyableFeature u-marginTopXSmall u-paddingBottomXSmall <div class="section firstReview">\n      \n\n<div id="review_1041428588" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1041428588" />\n    <a title="Andrew Garvin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27957434-andrew-garvin"><img alt="Andrew Garvin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390443155p2/27957434.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1041428588?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2014</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Andrew Garvin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew Garvin" href="/user/show/27957434-andrew-garvin">Andrew Garvin</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1041428588" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer13080833161196491972">Having worked with Peter - and the PayPal mafia more generally - for almost 10 years now, I have a unique perspective on Zero to One. Many of the ideas contained within are familiar to me. The launching point for the book is Peter&apos;s stock interview question - a question he asked me 8 years ago.<br/><br/>&apos;What is something you think is true, but that most people disagree with you on?&apos;<br/><br/>My answer at the time (2006) was: &apos;There is a bubble in housing.&apos; Of course, that was cheating, since I knew this was a pet </span>\n  <span id="freeText13080833161196491972" style="display:none">Having worked with Peter - and the PayPal mafia more generally - for almost 10 years now, I have a unique perspective on Zero to One. Many of the ideas contained within are familiar to me. The launching point for the book is Peter\'s stock interview question - a question he asked me 8 years ago.<br /><br />\'What is something you think is true, but that most people disagree with you on?\'<br /><br />My answer at the time (2006) was: \'There is a bubble in housing.\' Of course, that was cheating, since I knew this was a pet idea of Peter\'s. Not surprisingly, he asked if I had another answer. I didn\'t, but I extemp-ed some bs about how FDI in Iraq was a good idea. Luckily I was strong enough in other ways to make it through.<br /><br />His breakdown of this question and why it\'s so difficult to answer is incisive and in itself reveals a truth that is lying in plain sight. Very few ideas are both contrarian and correct. And, yet, finding that combination is critical to successful investing, entrepreneurship, or even career development. <br /><br />The book moves at a quick, clear pace with examples generously distributed throughout. Even the Bible quotes (and nerd Bible quotes - i.e. Lord of the Rings) feel natural or at least refreshing. Moreover, as Peter is wont to do, there are several easter eggs or dog whistles in the text which hint at some of Peter\'s more unique ideas. Of course I am biased, but particularly in the \'business\' category, Zero to One is a special and thought-provoking book.</span>\n  <a data-text-id="13080833161196491972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border </div>\n    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>\n\n\n\n  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >\n    <div class="section">\n      \n\n<div id="review_1136670683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1136670683" />\n    <a title="Jacek Ambroziak" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7910716-jacek-ambroziak"><img alt="Jacek Ambroziak" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332967717p2/7910716.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1136670683?book_show_action=true">Dec 20, 2014</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Jacek Ambroziak" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jacek Ambroziak" href="/user/show/7910716-jacek-ambroziak">Jacek Ambroziak</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1136670683" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer556915557121918167">I thoroughly enjoyed the book even if I have found myself in violent disagreement with many of its thoughts. The book opens up with these words.<br/><br/><blockquote>&quot;Every moment in business happens only once.<br/><br/>The next Bill Gates will not build an operating system. The next Larry Page or Sergey Brin won\xe2\x80\x99t make a search engine. And the next Mark Zuckerberg won\xe2\x80\x99t create a social network. If you are copying these guys, you aren\xe2\x80\x99t learning from them.<br/><br/>It\xe2\x80\x99s easier to copy a model than to make something new: doing what we al</blockquote></span>\n  <span id="freeText556915557121918167" style="display:none">I thoroughly enjoyed the book even if I have found myself in violent disagreement with many of its thoughts. The book opens up with these words.<br /><br /><blockquote>"Every moment in business happens only once.<br /><br />The next Bill Gates will not build an operating system. The next Larry Page or Sergey Brin won\xe2\x80\x99t make a search engine. And the next Mark Zuckerberg won\xe2\x80\x99t create a social network. If you are copying these guys, you aren\xe2\x80\x99t learning from them.<br /><br />It\xe2\x80\x99s easier to copy a model than to make something new: doing what we already know how to do takes the world from 1 to n, adding more of something familiar. But every time we create something new, we go from 0 to 1. The act of creation is singular, as is the moment of creation, and the result is something fresh and strange."</blockquote><br /><br />Initially, I kind of liked this quote. It is indeed hard to imagine a new business to threaten the dominance of Google Search or Facebook\'s social network. But the fact that Google search dominates is not really a zero-to-one effect, but a decisive improvement over previously existing internet search engines. Facebook was not originally conceived of as a dominant social platform; it so happened that it turned out to be well liked by millions of people beyond the original community of Harvard students. In operating systems we already have Linux and Android (using Linux) which have way more deployments than Windows. So it is not the case that "every moment in business" happens only once, unless in a very literal sense.<br /><br />Peter discusses "copying" with disdain, but not all "copying" is just copying, a lot of progress happens via semi-continuous improvements. This civilization does not only progress by one-off disruptive inventions. A lot of it is steady improvements. And these improvements occasionally lead to <i>enabling</i> zero-to-one (or dominating) developments.<br /><br />Peter asks "What company is nobody building?" Like the previous quote, this question has some charm, but I think it too is misleading. Well, maybe nobody is building this "company X" yet, because it is not yet <i>fully enabled</i>. Eg. voice over internet existed in the lab (IBM?) since mid 70\'s, but Skype made sense only much later when lots of people got access to personal computers and these computers started being connected to the Net all the time. <br /><br />Bottom line: it is not all "zero to one", nor it can be or should be.<br /><br />I still give the book 5 stars as I enjoyed its thought provoking character; it was an engaging reading: disagreeing, agreeing, learning new perspectives. Highly recommended but please don\'t treat it as Gospel :-)</span>\n  <a data-text-id="556915557121918167" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1136670683">\n  <td colspan="2">\n    <div class="updateActionLinks">\n        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link1136670683" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=1136670683&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18050143-zero-to-one">flag</a>\n\n\n\n\n\n      <span class=\'likeItContainer\' class=\'brown_comment\'>\n<span class="loadingLinkSpan"><a class="loadingLink" data-remote="true" href="/review/1136670683/comments?reverse=true">View all 25 comments</a><span class="loadingAnimation"></span><span class="loadingError"></span></span>\n</div>\n\n</div>\n\n  </td>\n</tr>\n\n      </div>\n\n  </div>\n\n  <div class="clear"></div>\n</div>\n\n    </div>\n  </div>\n    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>\n\n\n\n  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >\n    <div class="section">\n      \n\n<div id="review_1022112379" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1022112379" />\n    <a title="Yevgeniy Brikman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1363537-yevgeniy-brikman"><img alt="Yevgeniy Brikman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507385887p2/1363537.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1022112379?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2014</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Yevgeniy Brikman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Yevgeniy Brikman" href="/user/show/1363537-yevgeniy-brikman">Yevgeniy Brikman</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1022112379" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer14789164980561550725">This book fluctuates between brilliance and madness. When it focuses on the mechanics of start ups, it&apos;s great. When it focuses on Thiel&apos;s philosophies, it&apos;s a bit whacky. Thiel enjoys being a contrarian too much. Doing something new and valuable may require being a contrarian, but just being contrarian doesn&apos;t mean your ideas are new and valuable. Worth reading if you&apos;re interested in startups, but be prepared to skim and shake your head. <br/><br/>Pros:<br/><br/>* Great chapters on how to build a monopoly, approa</span>\n  <span id="freeText14789164980561550725" style="display:none">This book fluctuates between brilliance and madness. When it focuses on the mechanics of start ups, it\'s great. When it focuses on Thiel\'s philosophies, it\'s a bit whacky. Thiel enjoys being a contrarian too much. Doing something new and valuable may require being a contrarian, but just being contrarian doesn\'t mean your ideas are new and valuable. Worth reading if you\'re interested in startups, but be prepared to skim and shake your head. <br /><br />Pros:<br /><br />* Great chapters on how to build a monopoly, approach markets, luck, hiring, culture, and sales.<br /><br />* lots of contrarian views that will force you to reconsider your own ideas<br /><br />* Interesting outlook on the future of technology and humanity<br /><br />* Clear writing<br /><br />Cons:<br /><br />* The ideas of vertical vs horizontal progress is nonsense. All ideas are horizontal, built incrementally on top of all the ideas that came before by people who came along at the right time and place. This includes the ideas behind paypal and palantir. <br /><br />* Limited perspective on competition. It exists. It leads to better products for consumers. It hurts some businesses, but drives others to greatness. <br /><br />* I disagree with Thiel\'s negative view of education. Yes, higher ed is too expensive and can be done better, but that\'s not the same as eliminating it. And if you want to change it, then have your companies stop filtering candidates by college degree. <br /><br />* Dismissing a broad curriculum and saying everyone should study just one thing is absurd coming from a lawyer turned businessman who likes to quote a very wide array of human knowledge, including philosophy, history, physics, mathematics, medicine, economics, and mythology. It\'s also absurd since the fusion of ideas from different disciplines is what leads to much of innovation. <br /><br />* Comparing hipsters to the uni bomber? Really?<br /><br />Fun quotes:<br /><br />As a good rule of thumb, proprietary technology must be at least 10 times better than its closest substitute in some important dimension to lead to a real monopolistic advantage. Anything less than an order of magnitude better will probably be perceived as a marginal improvement and will be hard to sell, especially in an already crowded market.<br /><br />By the time a student gets to college, he\xe2\x80\x99s spent a decade curating a bewilderingly diverse r\xc3\xa9sum\xc3\xa9 to prepare for a completely unknowable future. Come what may, he\xe2\x80\x99s ready\xe2\x80\x94for nothing in particular.<br /><br />But leanness is a methodology, not a goal. Making small changes to things that already exist might lead you to a local maximum, but it won\xe2\x80\x99t help you find the global maximum. You could build the best version of an app that lets people order toilet paper from their iPhone. But iteration without a bold plan won\xe2\x80\x99t take you from 0 to 1. A company is the strangest place of all for an indefinite optimist: why should you expect your own business to succeed without a plan to make it happen? Darwinism may be a fine theory in other contexts, but in startups, intelligent design works best.<br /><br />As globalization advances, people perceive the world as one homogeneous, highly competitive marketplace: the world is \xe2\x80\x9cflat.\xe2\x80\x9d Given that assumption, anyone who might have had the ambition to look for a secret will first ask himself: if it were possible to discover something new, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t someone from the faceless global talent pool of smarter and more creative people have found it already? This voice of doubt can dissuade people from even starting to look for secrets in a world that seems too big a place for any individual to contribute something unique.<br /><br />The best entrepreneurs know this: every great business is built around a secret that\xe2\x80\x99s hidden from the outside. A great company is a conspiracy to change the world; when you share your secret, the recipient becomes a fellow conspirator.<br /><br />Every great company is unique, but there are a few things that every business must get right at the beginning. I stress this so often that friends have teasingly nicknamed it \xe2\x80\x9cThiel\xe2\x80\x99s law\xe2\x80\x9d: a startup messed up at its foundation cannot be fixed.<br /><br />You can\xe2\x80\x99t accomplish anything meaningful by hiring an interior decorator to beautify your office, a \xe2\x80\x9chuman resources\xe2\x80\x9d consultant to fix your policies, or a branding specialist to hone your buzzwords. \xe2\x80\x9cCompany culture\xe2\x80\x9d doesn\xe2\x80\x99t exist apart from the company itself: no company has a culture; every company is a culture. A startup is a team of people on a mission, and a good culture is just what that looks like on the inside.<br /><br />All salesmen are actors: their priority is persuasion, not sincerity.<br /><br />The most fundamental reason that even businesspeople underestimate the importance of sales is the systematic effort to hide it at every level of every field in a world secretly driven by it.<br /><br />It\xe2\x80\x99s better to think of distribution as something essential to the design of your product. If you\xe2\x80\x99ve invented something new but you haven\xe2\x80\x99t invented an effective way to sell it, you have a bad business\xe2\x80\x94no matter how good the product.<br /><br />Tthe seven questions that every business must answer:<br /><br />1. The Engineering Question<br />Can you create breakthrough technology instead of incremental improvements?<br /><br />2. The Timing Question<br />Is now the right time to start your particular business?<br /><br />3. The Monopoly Question<br />Are you starting with a big share of a small market?<br /><br />4. The People Question<br />Do you have the right team?<br /><br />5. The Distribution Question<br />Do you have a way to not just create but deliver your product?<br /><br />6. The Durability Question<br />Will your market position be defensible 10 and 20 years into the future?<br /><br />7. The Secret Question<br />Have you identified a unique opportunity that others don\'t see?</span>\n  <a data-text-id="14789164980561550725" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1022112379">\n  <td colspan="2">\n    <div class="updateActionLinks">\n        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link1022112379" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=1022112379&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18050143-zero-to-one">flag</a>\n\n\n\n\n\n      <span class=\'likeItContainer\' id=\'like_container_review_1022112379\'><a id="like_count_review_1022112379" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1022112379?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">208 href="/review/show/1022112379?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>\n    </div>\n\n\n    <div id=\'comments_for_review_1022112379\' style=\'display: none;\'>\n<div class=\'brown_comment\'>\n<span class="loadingLinkSpan"><a class="loadingLink" data-remote="true" href="/review/1022112379/comments?reverse=true">View all 7 comments</a><span class="loadingAnimation"></span><span class="loadingError"></span></span>\n</div>\n\n</div>\n\n  </td>\n</tr>\n\n      </div>\n\n  </div>\n\n  <div class="clear"></div>\n</div>\n\n    </div>\n  </div>\n    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>\n\n\n\n  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >\n    <div class="section">\n      \n\n<div id="review_915062535" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/915062535" />\n    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6446977-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454783581p2/6446977.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/915062535?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2014</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/6446977-nick">Nick</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n                  <em>\n                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,\n                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer915062535&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>\n                  </em>\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer915062535" class="readable"\n                    style="display:none"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer15730907156908551573">The first book since Antifragile that had me hooked beginning to end. The definite/ indefinite paradigm had me thinking long after the book was finished. Fantastic.<br/><br/>Ch. 1 The challenge of the the future<br/><br/>The first chapter is an introductory piece on the creation of new value (going from nothing to something) thus zero to one as opposed to logarithmic and or incremental changes one to n. <br/><br/>- He argues that spreading old ways to create wealth around the world will only further globalize the world into </span>\n  <span id="freeText15730907156908551573" style="display:none">The first book since Antifragile that had me hooked beginning to end. The definite/ indefinite paradigm had me thinking long after the book was finished. Fantastic.<br /><br />Ch. 1 The challenge of the the future<br /><br />The first chapter is an introductory piece on the creation of new value (going from nothing to something) thus zero to one as opposed to logarithmic and or incremental changes one to n. <br /><br />- He argues that spreading old ways to create wealth around the world will only further globalize the world into sameness. This sameness means a universal current state. The downside to this is that everyone will now have a North American live style which requires a lot more energy and competition for the same resources. Thiel calls for a pursuit of new growth not a desire to spread the sameness around.<br /><br />- He argues that old technologies still pervade our society and that new technologies will and should come from startups because large scale bureaucracies move slowly and entrenched interests in these organizations shy away from risk. In a lot of organizations signaling that your work is completed is more important than the work itself.<br /><br />Ch. 2 <br /><br />- When growing a company exponential growth in size of your business also means exponential growth in your cost structure.<br /><br />- Out of fear of a post 90\'s tech bubble do not treat the lessons learned as dogma. The goal of lessons is that the mistakes of the past are potential future realities not a determined future. Silicon Valley currently is trapped in this thinking that the bubble of the past dictates how we should act presently. <br /><br />- Current tech companies focus on too much 1) incremental advances 2) have no idea where there business will go or what they will be doing 3) forego creating new markets and instead attempt to gain more ground in overcrowded markets 4) ignore sales completely<br /><br />- The opposite is important 1) risk boldness 2) have a framework of where you\'d like to take your company 3) search for new markets 4) sales matter as does product design not more though<br /><br />Ch. 3 All Happy Companies are Different<br /> <br />- Ask yourself what valuable company is nobody building.<br /><br />- Create value and then capture that value<br /><br />- Choose to become a monopoly as competitive markets strip your profits away. Become a company so good at what you do that no one offers a close substitute. Differentiate your company from all others.<br /><br />- Monopolists lies to protect themselves (google claims its an advertising company and thus has only 3% of the global advertising market but if it is to be considered a search engine company then it owns 67% of the search engine market).<br /><br />- Competitive companies lie and exaggerate their share of the market to attract investors.<br /><br />- Monopolistic profits allow companies to transcend the competitive struggle and focus on their employees.<br /><br />-Monopolies can keep innovating and profits ease long term planning and make it more feasible to attempt ambitious R&amp;D projects.<br /><br />- Basic economics are a relic of the past.<br /><br />- Every business is successful exactly to the extent that it does something others cannot.<br /><br />- Rivalry causes us to overemphasize old opportunities and slavishly copy what has worked in the past.<br /><br />- Is your market the right one to be in? Everybody loses when the war isn\'t worth fighting.<br /><br />- Do not accumulate enemies. And if you can\'t beat a rival consider merging.<br /><br />- Short term thinking diverts your focus from company growth and durability. <br /><br />- Will your company be around in 10 years?<br /><br />- Characteristics that may guarantee you dominance: proprietary technology, network effects, economies of scale, and brand. <br /><br />- The problem with MBA types: initial markets are small that they often do not perceive them as opportunities.<br /><br />- Choose small market demand with immediate product adoption.<br /><br />- First dominate a specific niche and then scale to adjacent markets.<br /><br />Chapter 6:<br /><br />- How you perceive the future may dictate your outcome. Are you indefinitely pessimistic, definitely pessimistic, definitely optimistic or indefinitely optimistic. <br /><br />- Indefinite pessimist: the future is bleak and there is nothing anyone can do about it. <br /><br />- Definite pessimist: the future is bleak and must be prepared for.<br /><br />- Definite optimist: the future will be better and everyone must work in a direction to make it better (Entrepeneurs)<br /><br />- Indefinite optimist: the future will be better but how it will materialize is unforeseeable (law school students).<br /><br />- Entrepeneurs do not view their future as out of their hands and are optimistic enough to plan for their future. They only sell their company when they have no plans for where they would like to take it. <br /><br />- Long term planning has become undervalued in a world that only thinks in quarterly cycles and 4 year election cycles.<br /><br />- A startup is the largest endeavour over which you can have some sort of control over your own destiny.<br /><br />Ch. 7<br /><br />- Venture returns don\'t follow a normal distribution, rather they follow a power law: a small handful of companies outperform all others.<br /><br />- The biggest secret in venture capital is that the best investment in a successful fund equals or outperforms the entire rest of fund combined. Every investment in your portfolio must have the potential to succeed at a vast scale. Less than 1% of companies started each year receive venture funding. <br /><br />- You are an investor in yourself. When you choose a career you believe your line of work will be desired in years to come.<br /><br />- An individual cannot diversify his own life by keeping dozens of possible careers in his back pocket. <br /><br />Ch. 8<br /><br />- What company is nobody building? <br /><br />- Have unorthodox ideas and do not be afraid to hold these ideas. Having mainstream ideas that everyone else has is not a sign of progress. It is thinking with a contrarian-esk perception is where new ideas stem from.<br /><br />- If you think something is hard you\'ll never try. <br /><br />Ch.9 Foundations<br /><br />- Thiel\'s law: a startup messed up at the beginning cannot be fixed.<br /><br />- Bad decisions made early on such as choosing the wrong partners or hiring the wrong people can be very hard to correct after they are made.<br /><br />- You cannot build something great on flawed foundations.<br /><br />- When you start something the most important decision is who you start it with.<br /><br />- Technical abilities and complementary skill sets matter, but how well the founders know each other and how well they work together matter just as much. Founders should have a prehistory before they start a company together otherwise it is just a gamble.<br /><br />- The benefit of working for yourself is you have sole control of a vision the downside is that you don\'t gain the benefit of having a team.<br /><br />- You need good people who get along but you also need a structure to keep everyone aligned for the long term.<br /><br />- Sources of unalignment: ownership, possession and/ or control. Ownership: who owns equity. Possession: who runs the company on a day to day basis. Control: who governs the company\'s affairs.<br /><br />- Be aware when bureaucracy is creeping into your company. <br /><br />- Refrain from having conflict between investors and founders due to differing interests and priorities. Have a smaller board because the smaller the board the easier it is for people to reach consensus and for control to be exercised. Every person on your board matters because each has the possibility to bring an issue that you may have to deal with so be selective. A board of 3 to 5 is perfect.<br /><br />- Everyone should be a full time employee (except lawyers and accountants) to keep your company aligned with your goals.<br /><br />- A company does better the less its CEO makes. A CEO that takes a high salary in startup will defend his pay and the status quo while a CEO that takes less money or money equal to his founders and employees will work hard to ensure problems do not arise and when problems arise to help solve them. A cash poor executive will continually focus on creating value for his or her company.<br /><br />- Anyone who prefers owning a part of your company rather than being paid in cash reveals a preference for the long term and a commitment to increasing your company\'s value.<br /><br />- The most valuable kind of company maintains an openness to invention and new ideas.<br /><br />Ch. 10<br /><br />-Do not assemble a team by hiring the most talented people based on a resume or some other arbitrary metric of talent.<br /><br />- Your time is your most valuable asset and thus create a team you would enjoy spending time with on a shared vision. You want a tight knit group not a team of free agents only looking out for themselves. Hire people who enjoy working together, excited about your vision and talented. <br /><br />- Always ask yourself why someone would want to work for you not the inverse which is why should I hire them. If they ask why they should work for you you should be able to respond why your mission matters and why your team is a team people likeminded people where they could not afford to not want to join. <br /><br />- Make every person responsible for doing just one thing by defining roles to reduce conflict. Avoid people overlapping each other doing the same job and thus leading to conflict over the same responsibilities. Eliminate competition among your employees by differentiating their tasks.<br /><br />Ch. 11<br /><br />- Never underestimate the importance of sales. Sales works best when hidden. The worst salesmen are the ones who let us know we are being sold to.<br /><br />- A product is viral if its core functionality encourages users to invite their friends to become users to. <br /><br />- You must also sell your company to investors and employees. Keep them engaged. <br /><br />- You should never assume that people will admire your company without a public relations strategy.<br /><br />Ch. 12<br /><br />- Computers are complements for humans not rivals or substitutes. The most valuable businesses of the future will be created by entrepreneurs who seek to empower people rather than making people obsolete. <br /><br />Ch. 13<br /><br />- Companies should strive to make their product 10X better than their rivals because merely incremental improvements often end up meaning no real improvement at all for the end user.<br /><br />- Entering a slow moving market can be a good strategy but only if you have a definite and realistic plan to take it over. <br /><br />- Customers won\'t care about any particular technology unless it solves a particular problem in a superior way.And if you can\'t monopolize a unique solution for a small market you will be stuck with vicious competition. <br /><br />Ask yourself 7 questions<br /><br />1) Is your technology superior?<br />2) Is the timing right?<br />3) Do you have a monopolistic advantage?<br />4) Have you assembled a good team?<br />5) Do you have a good distribution chain?<br />6) How durable is your company?<br />7) Do you have any insight as to why your company is different?<br /><br />Ch. 14<br /><br />- The most important task in business is the creation of new value which cannot be reduced to a formula.<br /><br />- We need unusual individuals to lead companies beyond mere incrementalism.<br /><br />Ch. 15<br /><br />- Always remember the first essential step is to think for yourself.</span>\n  <a data-text-id="15730907156908551573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review915062535">\n  <td colspan="2">\n    <div class="updateActionLinks">\n        <a class="right bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1360707711?book_show_action=true">Aug 10, 2015</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="James Fairbairn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Fairbairn" href="/user/show/29617641-james-fairbairn">James Fairbairn</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360707711" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer8626478340602853776">Starts well, becomes trite, ends delusional.</span>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1360707711">\n  <td colspan="2">\n    <div class="updateActionLinks">\n        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link1360707711" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=1360707711&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18050143-zero-to-one">flag</a>\n\n\n\n\n\n      <span class=\'likeItContainer\' id=\'like_container_review_1360707711\'><a id="like_count_review_1360707711" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1360707711?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">58 class=\'brown_comment\'>\n<span class="loadingLinkSpan"><a class="loadingLink" data-remote="true" href="/review/1360707711/comments?reverse=true">View 1 comment</a><span </div>\n    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>\n\n\n\n  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >\n    <div class="section">\n      \n\n<div id="review_1493095259" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1493095259" />\n    <a title="Jamie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1385106-jamie"><img alt="Jamie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1217535570p2/1385106.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1493095259?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2016</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Jamie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie" href="/user/show/1385106-jamie">Jamie</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1493095259" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer10110993470784108487">I hate this book and I suspect that the feeling is mutual.<br/><br/>Zero to One is a bunch of thoughts about entrepreneurs and startups by Peter Thiel, co-founder of Pay Pal. The book doesn&apos;t have a central thesis or framework like many other business advice books. Rather, each chapter is more or less self contained set of thoughts about how someone starting a company should to go from &quot;zero to one&quot; (create something new where nothing like it existed before) instead of &quot;one to two&quot; (iterating on something</span>\n  <span id="freeText10110993470784108487" style="display:none">I hate this book and I suspect that the feeling is mutual.<br /><br />Zero to One is a bunch of thoughts about entrepreneurs and startups by Peter Thiel, co-founder of Pay Pal. The book doesn\'t have a central thesis or framework like many other business advice books. Rather, each chapter is more or less self contained set of thoughts about how someone starting a company should to go from "zero to one" (create something new where nothing like it existed before) instead of "one to two" (iterating on something or copying some existing service or product). There\'s a chapter on the 1999/2000 dot com boom, there\'s a chapter where Thiel shares his contempt for competing in an open market, a chapter on company culture, and more.<br /><br />It\'s a bunch of nonsense that leans more towards being a polemic than a business advice book. It\'s mainly Thiel looking down on what he sees as the problems of the business world and trying to convince you that he has all the answers to problems nobody else has even thought of. This is super aggravating, since he is so partial to misstating an opposing point of view and making strawman arguments. He does it, for example, in the chapter about competition and monopolies where he brushes aside the generally accepted definitions of monopolies and competition and instead hacks them into new shapes to fit his arguments. <br /><br />My biggest "this is BS" realization came with the chapter "You are not a lottery ticket." This was his examination of Malcom Gladwell\'s "luck plays a role in success" idea from the book Outliers. Thiel says that the serial entrepreneurship of a handful of people --many of them rich from previous successes or otherwise privileged-- is grounds for dismissing the role of luck or good timing in success. It\'s a dishonest and self-serving examination of the topic that ignores the fact that Outliers (and the work it was based on) all account for motivation and ability as well as luck. It\'s the worse, lazy, anecdotal, self-serving kind of thinking. <br /><br />And once you realize that Thiel is out to misattribute, redefine, and cherry pick anecdotes in order to prop himself and his arguments up, you see it everywhere and you can no longer take anything he says at face value. He claims, for example, that nothing but information technology and communications technology have evolved much since the 70s. Which has to be untrue. We have made crazy advances in all kinds of fields --medicine, astrophysics, chemistry. I mean, are all those Nobel prizes undeserved? Thiel doesn\'t have the desire to research outside of his own world and so just makes proclamations that support his thesis at the moment.<br /><br />Another example of Thiel just saying something to make it true: "This is why physics PhDs are notoriously difficult to work with --because they know the most fundamental truths, they think they know all the truths." (pg 104). Uh, [citation needed]. I could see an author making the same point by telling a story about a physicist (or some other scientist) he once worked with and found to be a pain in the ass. Then saying "people like Dave tend to..." Another writer may talk about the Dunning Kruger effect or about research showing how expertise in one field biases you in others. But no. Thiel is all in. He\'s too impressed with his own insights to do anything but write them down and move on.<br /><br />Really, this is the work of a crank. It\'s more coherent and better written mechanically than a messageboard screed, but it\'s still the work of a crank. On page 96 he equates hipsters with the unibomber and religious fundamentalists. He\'s serious. The book has nothing to offer but platitudes and advice that\'s impossible to actually act on.<br /></span>\n  <a data-text-id="10110993470784108487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1493095259">\n  <td colspan="2">\n    <div class="updateActionLinks">\n        <a class="right flag" rel="nofollow" id="flag_link1493095259" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=1493095259&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18050143-zero-to-one">flag</a>\n\n\n\n\n\n      <span class=\'likeItContainer\' id=\'like_container_review_1493095259\'><a id="like_count_review_1493095259" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1493095259?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">49 class="loadingAnimation"></span><span class="loadingError"></span></span>\n</div>\n\n</div>\n\n  </td>\n</tr>\n\n      </div>\n\n  </div>\n\n  <div class="clear"></div>\n</div>\n\n    </div>\n  </div>\n    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>\n\n\n\n  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >\n    <div class="section">\n      \n\n<div id="review_1057030597" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1057030597" />\n    <a title="Otis Chandler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1-otis-chandler"><img alt="Otis Chandler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1506617226p2/1.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1057030597?book_show_action=true">Dec 22, 2018</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Otis Chandler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Otis Chandler" href="/user/show/1-otis-chandler">Otis Chandler</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>\n\n          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>\n          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22047640-zero-to-one">review of another edition</a>\n\n\n          <div>\n    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Otis by: </span>\n      <a href="/user/show/74273750-ted-janus">Ted Janus</a>\n  </div>\n\n\n          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">\n            Shelves:\n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1-otis-chandler?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, \n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1-otis-chandler?shelf=finance">finance</a>, \n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1-otis-chandler?shelf=economics">economics</a>\n          </div>\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1057030597" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer980717597166356405">A fascinating book, great for investing and entrepreneurs. I like how he really focuses on contrarian thinking, and his mental models for it. But only giving it 4 stars because I wish he went deeper there, with more examples. But this quote is gold:<br/><br/><a href=\'https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/82b228bc-fb61-41ba-9895-c6ec8dbb24f2?ref=rnlp\' rel=\'nofollow noopener\'>&quot;The most contrarian thing of all is not to oppose the crowd but to think for yourself.&quot;</a> <br/><br/>Jeff Bezos says this often - to really create something new and innovate, copying others doesn&apos;t typically work. What works is Customer Obsession - focusing obses</span>\n  <span id="freeText980717597166356405" style="display:none">A fascinating book, great for investing and entrepreneurs. I like how he really focuses on contrarian thinking, and his mental models for it. But only giving it 4 stars because I wish he went deeper there, with more examples. But this quote is gold:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/82b228bc-fb61-41ba-9895-c6ec8dbb24f2?ref=rnlp" rel="nofollow noopener">"The most contrarian thing of all is not to oppose the crowd but to think for yourself."</a> <br /><br />Jeff Bezos says this often - to really create something new and innovate, copying others doesn\'t typically work. What works is Customer Obsession - focusing obsessively on solving problems people have. If you do that, you have a chance to create something truly innovative. If you are focused on competition - the "hot space", the "hot technology", the "hot market" - you likely will create something marginal. Goodreads was born and evolved this way - by maniacally focusing on solving problems for book lovers.<br /><br />One of the more interesting points in the book, is that we live in a competitive society, and that causes us to over-optimize on the wrong things. We instead focus on beating the next guy or company instead of truly innovating, or focusing on what really matters to humans. In each company our goals and career growth define our pay, and thus we have to beat our coworkers, and our competitors to be judged successful. But even worse, competitive thinking is not creative thinking, and so it tends to be very incremental and built on past ideas, rather than creative and innovative. It also tends to be short term vs long term. Two quotes around this:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/e0b569ac-9a4d-471c-93f1-159d8132eb5e?ref=rnlp" rel="nofollow noopener">"More than anything else, competition is an ideology\xe2\x80\x94the ideology\xe2\x80\x94that pervades our society and distorts our thinking. We preach competition, internalize its necessity, and enact its commandments; and as a result, we trap ourselves within it\xe2\x80\x94even though the more we compete, the less we gain."</a><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/3e5b89f7-6aa2-4ae1-993a-048f86498bcf?ref=rnlp" rel="nofollow noopener">"Rivalry causes us to overemphasize old opportunities and slavishly copy what has worked in the past."</a><br /><br />Another point I liked that sounds obvious but is actually fairly contrarian in practice is that startups are most successful when focused on small markets, but ones that have high concentration, so the product can grow quickly in it. Most MBA\xe2\x80\x99s will ignore these markets because they are so small. In Goodreads\xe2\x80\x99s case, our initial set of reviewers were not a huge number of people, but they were very concentrated in the 2007 era blogging platforms - they had all their friends on their "blog rolls", and thus when they discovered us we grew rather quickly in those communities. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/da66adb9-7cb3-442c-a75c-a49980cd96c2?ref=rnlp" rel="nofollow noopener">"The perfect target market for a startup is a small group of particular people concentrated together and served by few or no competitors. Any big market is a bad choice, and a big market already served by competing companies is even worse. This is why it\xe2\x80\x99s always a red flag when entrepreneurs talk about getting 1% of a $100 billion market. In practice, a large market will either lack a good starting point or it will be open to competition, so it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to ever reach that 1%."&gt;<br /><br />I loved the section on how in technology sales is often underestimated. This is in part because nerds have a natural aversion to sales, preferring to think all problems can be solved by technology. It turns out, they are only half right - good sales is half the battle, sometimes more. Another part of this stems from how nobody likes to think they are being "sold to" - for this reason, nobody whose job it is to sell has the word "sales" in it anymore - now we have account executives, BD, investment bankers, politicians, etc. <br /><br />Tips to remember before investing in a company:<br />- How well do the founders know each other, will they work well together. Study them.<br />- How much does the CEO pay himself - shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be too high<br />- Are they trying to compete in a marginalized way or trying to invent something new?<br /><br />I have noticed a pervasive sentiment among a number of people who work in technology that "all the problems have been solved, or will be by large companies". Peter is optimistic on this front, saying </a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/811abd4c-c4a3-44f4-9083-3c0777c677cf" rel="nofollow noopener">"there are many more secrets left to find"</a>. Interestingly, he seems to focus more on scientific innovation in his list than informational or marketplaces. <br /><br />But I liked this quote perhaps best - it really summarizes what I took from the book. Doing something different *is* likely whats going to truly help society the most, AND create value. That is an interesting thing to ponder!<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/notes/37653154-zero-to-one/1-otis-chandler/22fdaccb-9d15-481f-9b0c-bbbc67546cfd?ref=rnlp" rel="nofollow noopener">"Doing something different is what\xe2\x80\x99s truly good for society\xe2\x80\x94and it\xe2\x80\x99s also what allows a business to profit by monopolizing a new market. The best projects are likely to be overlooked, not trumpeted by a crowd; the best problems to work on are often the ones nobody else even tries to solve."</a></span>\n  <a data-text-id="980717597166356405" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n id="flag_link1057030597" title="Flag this review as inappropriate." href="/flagged/new?resource_id=1057030597&amp;resource_type=Review&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18050143-zero-to-one">flag</a>\n\n\n\n\n\n      <span class=\'likeItContainer\' id=\'like_container_review_1057030597\'><a id="like_count_review_1057030597" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1057030597?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">55 href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F18050143-zero-to-one">Like</a></span>\n&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a href="/review/show/1057030597?book_show_action=true&amp;from_review_page=1">see review</a>\n    </div>\n\n\n    <div id=\'comments_for_review_1057030597\' style=\'display: none;\'>\n<div class=\'brown_comment\'>\n<span class="loadingLinkSpan"><a class="loadingLink" data-remote="true" href="/review/1057030597/comments?reverse=true">View all 3 comments</a><span class="loadingAnimation"></span><span class="loadingError"></span></span>\n</div>\n\n</div>\n\n  </td>\n</tr>\n\n      </div>\n\n  </div>\n\n  <div class="clear"></div>\n</div>\n\n    </div>\n  </div>\n    <div class="elementListBrownSeparator"></div>\n\n\n\n  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >\n    <div class="section">\n      \n\n<div id="review_1134761631" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1134761631" />\n    <a title="Sindy Li" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10578555-sindy-li"><img alt="Sindy Li" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349889794p2/10578555.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1134761631?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2014</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Sindy Li" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sindy Li" href="/user/show/10578555-sindy-li">Sindy Li</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">\n            Shelves:\n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10578555-sindy-li?shelf=technology">technology</a>, \n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10578555-sindy-li?shelf=business">business</a>\n          </div>\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1134761631" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer4643596428995188064">I heard about this book when it came out and thought that there was no way I would read a book on startups. Not that I don&apos;t see great things coming out of some startups, but I am not the only one who has developed a fatigue of the many random startups founded by fellow Silicon Valley dwellers who are engineers, MBAs, or a little bit of both, and of the many other Valley dwellers who claim they want to do a startup without knowing what it will be about!<br/><br/>After my dad bought a copy in Taipei I deci</span>\n  <span id="freeText4643596428995188064" style="display:none">I heard about this book when it came out and thought that there was no way I would read a book on startups. Not that I don\'t see great things coming out of some startups, but I am not the only one who has developed a fatigue of the many random startups founded by fellow Silicon Valley dwellers who are engineers, MBAs, or a little bit of both, and of the many other Valley dwellers who claim they want to do a startup without knowing what it will be about!<br /><br />After my dad bought a copy in Taipei I decided to take a look. I ended up enjoying it much more than I expected. This is a book of solid, sensible advice on startups, coming from economic principles and common sense. (I have no experience and little knowledge of startups, so I am merely speaking from an economist\'s perspective.) I wouldn\'t generalize Thiel\'s wisdom to fields outside of startups (just like the case with Paul Graham) -- indeed he made some claims that were not well thought out -- but the main points of the book were valuable. I would recommend it especially to those currently or thinking of working in a startup, and hopefully this will lead to less and on average better startups being founded.<br /><br />Below are a few ideas I liked from the book. They are not necessarily original but are pretty good. Note that I read the Chinese version and translated the phrases back into English so they are probably different from the book.<br /><br />Chapter 1: The difference between horizontal progress/globalization and vertical progress/technological innovation. The latter is needed to solve problems challenging our future. (However the former is not trivial!)<br /><br />Chapter 3: Innovation and unique technology that the market demands give you profitable monopoly power. Usually economists talk about some extent of monopoly power (e.g. patents) encourages innovation, but Thiel was looking at it from another perspective: that of an entrepreneur choosing which type of business to start. His answer is to choose one that makes differentiated products (that the market demands) and gives you monopoly power.<br /><br />Chapter 4: Again, competition vs. monopoly. Traditional businesses provide similar products and compete by cutting prices/costs, advertising etc.; innovative businesses make products that no one else makes.<br /><br />Chapter 5: What are the features of a business that can create and sustain monopoly power, growth and cash flow? 1. Unique technology (at least 10 times better than the existing alternative--otherwise it won\'t be noticed--or entirely new products). 2. Network externality. 3. Economies of scale. 4. Brand. How to create one? Start by monopolizing a small market (that needs to actually exist, unlike the market of British food in Palo Alto) through winning the most important group of users in this market, then expand in size or variety (i.e. into related markets). Don\'t focus on DISRUPTION; make the pie bigger instead of playing a zero sum game.<br /><br />Chapter 6: Have a purpose, a vision and long-run planning, instead of a lean startup and minimum viable product to be driven by whatever comes up on the way. This idea is related to the one on an "authoritarian" business presented in the last chapter. (The philosophical discussion in this chapter is not particularly great.)<br /><br />Chapter 7: Power law. As a venture capitalist, don\'t simply pursue diversification; choose a few businesses to invest in and choose them carefully so all of them have great (expected) potential. (Wait, did anyone derive optimal investment rules under power law distribution?)<br /><br />Chapter 8: (I like this one a lot.) All great business have "secrets". A world without secrets is boring, stagnated and has no room to improve upon. Our world is full of injustice and inefficiency, so it cannot be one without secrets. What prevent us from exploring these secrets are gradualism, risk aversion, inertia and belief in equilibrium ("flatness" or perfect efficiency of markets). Only those who see secrets can grasp hidden opportunities, lead to Scientific Revolutions and found businesses like Airbnb, Uber, Lyft etc. How to find secrets? Look where no one else does; choose the path less traveled. That\'s why it\'s (kinda) important to have contrarian views. (Though I think it\'s more important to be thinking than to have contrarian views for the sake of it. Thiel seems to agree by saying, at the end of Chapter 2, that the most unique way is not to be different from everyone else but to think for yourself.)<br /><br />Chapter 9: According to power law, there are a few things that are crucial to the entire business, e.g. its foundations. Make sure you choose founding partners who are really passionate about the business and you enjoy working together. (Stuff on the size of the board, stock as incentives etc.) You are not only trying to create new things at the founding stage of a startup; if you are successful, you should have created a business that stays creative.<br /><br />Chapter 10: The company doesn\'t attract employees (or create a "culture") by providing benefits like free food, free laundry etc. It should attract employees by what it does and who the team are. A company should be its own culture. It should be like a cult (rather than a consulting firm with no loyalty or identity), but one that is not extreme. (In Chapter 8, the HP example shows that once a company is managed in the conservative "MBA" way to optimize for bureaucratic functioning, innovation dies.)<br /><br />Chapter 11: Marketing is important. Marketing influences everyone, especially those who think they are not influenced. The best marketer doesn\'t look like one. Different ways of marketing, from viral marketing to complex sales. Make sure you have a viable marketing strategy for your product. It\'s great if your customers can market for you, e.g. through network externality (like PayPal).<br /><br />Chapter 12: Humans and computers should be complements (e.g. PayPal fraud detection, Palantir helping intelligence experts) rather than substitutes. (Great idea, and makes sense in some way--there are things that one is good at while the other is not--but in practice they are also substitutes in many ways which have unfortunate implications for employment.)<br /><br />Chapter 13: The failure of most clean technology firms was a business rather than political one. How Tesla succeeded while many others failed on 7 dimensions: engineering, timing, monopoly, personnel, marketing, sustainability, secrets. The fallacy of social entrepreneurship. (I think businesses that generate positive externalities are great but I agree that they must be self-sustaining and that being a "social enterprise" shouldn\'t be an excuse to not pursue commercial viability.)<br /><br />Chapter 14: Many (tech) entrepreneurs are "weird". Innovative tech companies are usually authoritarian with charismatic leaders like Steve Jobs. Society should be more tolerant of seemingly weird or extreme entrepreneurs, because we ned extraordinary people to lead companies in order to avoid the slow progress of gradualism. However, GLADLY, Thiel also advises such leaders to remain cautious, not to over estimate their power or become "prime movers" of Ayn Rand who do not realize that their success relies on other people.<br /><br />Lastly, I noticed one thing: most women who showed up in illustrations were (at least) half naked (model with Richard Branson, Britney Spears, Lady Gaga etc.). It\'s true that female entertainment celebrities usually appear with much less clothes than male ones, but having such pictures in a book on startups was quite annoying for a female reader, and probably distracting for a male one. Peter Thiel has just revealed another problem of Silicon Valley--sexism--in his book, except this time he did it not by words, but by action.</span>\n  <a data-text-id="4643596428995188064" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">\n  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1861277986" />\n    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63168028-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516912354p2/63168028.jpg" /></a>\n\n  <div class="left bodycol">\n    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">\n        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1861277986?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2018</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/63168028-peter">Peter</a>\n      </span>\n\n        rated it\n        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>\n\n\n\n        \n\n          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">\n            Shelves:\n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=business">business</a>, \n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=group-reads">group-reads</a>, \n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=business-group">business-group</a>, \n              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63168028-peter?shelf=top-20-business">top-20-business</a>\n          </div>\n    </div>\n\n\n        <div class="reviewText stacked">\n              <span id="reviewTextContainer1861277986" class="readable"\n              >\n            \n<span id="freeTextContainer1250390300271606615"><b>Singularity</b><br/>In business, we don\xe2\x80\x99t get the panacea methodology guaranteed to deliver a market-dominating business or monopoly, and the methodology for the next big thing, likely hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been defined yet. We need to look at each business from multiple perspectives and I do believe Peter Thiel provides another unique perspective \xe2\x80\x93 not a replacement perspective but an additional one. The research shows that disruptive innovation typically comes from new start companies and they tend to dominate a new </span>\n  <span id="freeText1250390300271606615" style="display:none"><b>Singularity</b><br />In business, we don\xe2\x80\x99t get the panacea methodology guaranteed to deliver a market-dominating business or monopoly, and the methodology for the next big thing, likely hasn\xe2\x80\x99t been defined yet. We need to look at each business from multiple perspectives and I do believe Peter Thiel provides another unique perspective \xe2\x80\x93 not a replacement perspective but an additional one. The research shows that disruptive innovation typically comes from new start companies and they tend to dominate a new market niche until they grow dramatically or are acquired. Iterative innovation in specific markets tends to be dominated by the incumbents, which are usually large multinational companies if the market is lucrative. So I agree with Peter Thiel, that new start businesses need to consider an order of magnitude value step change over existing solutions, to succeed, which is the zero to one transformation.<br /><blockquote>\n  <i>Doing what we already know how to do takes the world from 1 to n, adding more of something familiar. But every time we create something new, we go from 0 to 1. The act of creation is singular, as in the moment of creation, and the result is something fresh and strange.</i>\n</blockquote><br />Zero to One suggests a very different method from the lean-agile approach proposed by Steve Blank and Eric Ries in The Four Steps to the Epiphany and The Lean Startup respectively. They suggest that Customer Discovery, Validation, Creation and Building are the cornerstones of the startup approach. <br /><br />I believe we need to start with a vision of what a successful business would look like, and we need to see that it will be significantly different (10x) from existing competitive solutions. How do we get there? By understanding and executing a market entry path that is iteratively to build, test &amp; learn. I would also question Thiel\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion that only technology enables that step change. In the cited case of Facebook, there were multiple solutions offering social media platforms and it appears the leadership and marketing of Facebook, were more the decisive factors. We could even argue that Facebook is an example of the Eric Ries approach.<br /><br />The example of Paypal and Thiel\xe2\x80\x99s insights into the economy and the investment community around the DotCom boom and bust were very interesting. The investor expectations are a constant challenge as I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard from one investor that he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t get out of bed if a company wasn\xe2\x80\x99t turning over \xe2\x82\xac40million in 3 years and another saying if you showed me figures like that I\xe2\x80\x99d think I was working with unrealistic idiots. <br /><br />After the main point of vertical innovation is made, the book rambles and while the discussion points are interesting you often wonder what this has that to do with the main premise of the book. The book does feel a little unstructured and elements seem to be included as they were part of a lecture series rather than an integral part of a framework for achieving that 0 to 1 impact. <br /><br />I would recommend reading this book as it may encourage and inspire you to consider where you want to go with the company and its core solutions. It does, however, need to be tempered with the knowledge that other approaches exist and Peter Thiel may be wrong, at least in parts.</span>\n  <a data-text-id="1250390300271606615" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>\n\n          </span>\n        </div>\n\n\n      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">\n          \n\n<tr class="no_border href="/review/show/3388184740?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2020</a>\n\n      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">\n        <a title="\xe2\x98\x98Misericordia\xe2\x98\x98 \xe2\x9a\xa1\xcf\x9f\xe2\x9a\xa1\xe2\x9b\x88\xe2\x9a\xa1\xe2\x98\x81 \xe2\x9d\x87\xef\xb8\x8f\xe2\x9d\xa4\xe2\x9d\xa3" class="user" itemprop="url"
